    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      10th July 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 15th October 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 28th November 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>P2-type layered oxides are attractive cathode active materials for sodium-ion batteries, however, these materials typically suffer from detrimental Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings. In this work, we investigate the origin as well as the influence of the transition metal ratio on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in P2-type cathode materials. A combination of X-ray diffraction (XRD), neutron diffraction, advanced electrochemical methods, <span class="italic">operando</span> XRD and DFT calculations is applied to study Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. In P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a honeycomb Ni/Mn superstructure leads to charge ordering within the transition metal slab and pronounced Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings, causing distinct voltage jumps at specific sodium contents (<span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3). For P2-Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the Ni/Mn superstructure is disrupted, resulting in more complex charge orderings within the transition metal slab, partially suppressed Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings and an overall smoother potential profile. Based on our findings, guidelines to suppress Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in P2-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries are postulated and discussed with respect to electrochemical measurements of various transition metal compositions. These guidelines can serve to predict the tendency towards Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings for a given cathode composition or to design new cathode compositions for enhanced cycle life based on the absence of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect488"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Increasing energy generation from renewables is inevitable to reduce the impact of man-made climate change.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> With that, the need for energy storage is gaining importance.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2–4</span></sup></a> Today, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) play a key role in mobile and stationary energy storage,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,5</span></sup></a> however, LIBs contain critical raw materials in terms of availability and cost, such as cobalt, nickel, lithium, copper or graphite.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6–9</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), in which sodium replaces lithium as the ionic charge carrier, are currently being developed and commercialized as a sustainable and potentially cost-effective complementary technology to today's LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10–13</span></sup></a> SIBs typically utilize the same working principle as LIBs,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> however, critical raw materials are either replaced by more abundant and cost-effective materials or reduced in content.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,16</span></sup></a> On the anode side, hard carbon typically replaces graphite as the active material<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17–20</span></sup></a> and aluminium is applied as a current collector instead of copper.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,21,22</span></sup></a> As the electrolyte, typically, solutions of NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in carbonate solvents replace LiPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> equivalents.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,23,24</span></sup></a> On the cathode side, sodium-containing intercalation materials substitute lithium nickel cobalt manganese oxides (NCM) or lithium iron phosphate (LFP) as the active material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,15,25</span></sup></a> Overall, these measures may result in a significantly reduced bill of materials for SIBs and potentially more sustainable batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22,26–30</span></sup></a> Additionally, SIBs promise more advantages over today's LIBs in terms of low-temperature performance,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> power capability,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> safety, and transportation and storage.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31–33</span></sup></a> Furthermore, SIBs can be produced on established production equipment, which reduces necessary investment costs and enables a rapid scale-up of the technology (drop-in technology).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> Cumulatively, these aspects make SIBs a sustainable and cost-effective secondary battery technology if their performance can be further enhanced.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">So far, one of the main bottlenecks for SIBs remains the cathode active material. Currently, mainly three material classes are explored as potential cathode active materials for SIBs, namely Prussian-Blue Analogues (PBAs), sodium vanadium fluorophosphates (NVFP) and layered sodium transition metal oxides (Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,25</span></sup></a> Among these three broad families, the latter offers attractive potential and capacity, high (crystal) density and can be produced in a similar fashion as today's NCM for LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,25,34–36</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">These layered sodium transition metal oxides can be synthesized in an O3, P3 or P2-type structure according to Delmas' notation, where a letter is used as an abbreviation of the alkali metal ion environment (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, O = octahedral and P = trigonal prismatic) and a numeral is used to describe the number of transition metal oxygen slabs (MO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) necessary to describe the unit cell fully. A prime is used to indicate distortion of the ideal structure.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">In O3-type layered sodium transition metal oxides, octahedra of adjoining sodium sites share edges. Hence, in these materials the diffusion from one sodium site to another proceeds <span class="italic">via</span> an intermediate tetrahedral site (divacancy mechanism), leading to moderate Na conductivities.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38,39</span></sup></a> In P2-type layered sodium transition metal oxides, prisms of adjoining sodium sites share faces, enabling a single-vacancy mechanism with low activation energy, typically resulting in high diffusion rates.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Eventually diffusion rates are a function of the sodium content (vacancy concentration) and can be highly influenced by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">P2-type sodium transition metal oxides typically offer high power capability due to fast solid diffusion, high discharge potential and attractive reversible capacities.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> In this material class, P2-type Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is one of the most investigated sodium layered oxides, due to its attractive performance in terms of reversible capacity and mean discharge potential as well as its high power capability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,42</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Synthesis and electrochemistry of P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> were first reported by Jeff Dahn and co-workers.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43–45</span></sup></a> During reversible electrochemical desodiation at room temperature, P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> follows a solid-solution-like behaviour, maintaining the P2 structure for <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 1/3.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> For <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 1/3, a reversible two-phase reaction between P2-Na<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and O2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span>≈0</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> takes place.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> The potential profile typically exhibits distinct voltage jumps at sodium stoichiometries of <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3, which are associated with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44,46,47</span></sup></a> These potential jumps are enclosed by flat regions (indicating the transition between ordered and disordered sodium configurations) at 3.16 V, 3.31 V &amp; 3.56 V and 3.66 V &amp; 4.10 V corresponding to sodium stoichiometries of approximately <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 − <span class="italic">δ</span>, 1/2 ± <span class="italic">δ</span> and 1/3 ± <span class="italic">δ</span> with 0.09 ≳ <span class="italic">δ</span> ≳ 0.04, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44,48–50</span></sup></a> The corresponding Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings within the sodium slab have been elucidated using first principles calculations by Meng <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordered compounds are thermodynamically more stable compared to their disordered counterparts, which results in impeded solid diffusion in proximity to the composition of the ordering<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46,51</span></sup></a> and potentially limits the cycle life of the battery.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> Additionally, the potential plateaus caused by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering are challenging for the application of a battery management system (BMS).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit52"><sup><span class="sup_ref">52</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">If some of the nickel in P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is substituted by manganese to obtain P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, similar performance can be achieved, however, the potential profile is considerably smoother, hinting at largely suppressed Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53–57</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">In this work, we investigate the influence of the transition metal ratio on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode active materials for SIBs. Both materials were synthesized and thoroughly characterized, using advanced electrochemical and analytical techniques as well as first principles calculations, to demonstrate that Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is closely related to charge ordering within the transition metal slab. A general guideline to suppress Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering in P2-type cathode materials is postulated and evaluated against various P2-type sodium transition metal oxides and reports in the literature.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect603"><span class="a_heading">Crystal structure of P2-type Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1−<span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></h2>
      <span>In layered sodium transition metal oxides, transition metals are octahedrally coordinated by oxygen atoms. Edge-sharing MO<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra form MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs. These MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs are separated by sodium slabs. Sodium is preferentially located in a triangular prismatic or octahedral environment with six-fold coordination to the surrounding oxygen atoms of the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> As described above, P2-type Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> structures are characterized by a trigonal prismatic environment of sodium and two MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs within the unit cell.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit58"><sup><span class="sup_ref">58</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Due to similar X-ray scattering length of 3d transition metals,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit59"><sup><span class="sup_ref">59</span></sup></a> the crystal structure of P2-type sodium transition metal oxides as derived from X-ray diffraction (XRD) is typically indexed in space group <span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>/<span class="italic">mmc</span> (SG 194),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60–62</span></sup></a> here noted as a “small unit cell” (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1A–C</a>). In this structure model, all transition metals are located on the same 2a site, <span class="italic">e.g.</span>, C, in the hexagonal stacking sequence. Oxygen atoms are located on 4f sites following the ABBA stacking sequence. Sodium ions in the prismatic environment are located either on 2d sites sharing edges with adjacent MO<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra (Na<small><sub>e</sub></small>) or 2b sites sharing faces (Na<small><sub>f</sub></small>) with the adjacent MO<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra. Please note that the number of available sodium sites is twice the amount of transition metal sites. However, the simultaneous occupation of adjacent Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites would result in close proximity of sodium ions with shared prismatic faces and is therefore considered unlikely.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a> According to the principles by Linus Pauling, Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites are expected to be energetically more favourable than Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic representation of the crystal structure of P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> as reported in the literature. (A) Structure of P2-type sodium transition metal layered oxides as described in the small unit cell (<span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>/<span class="italic">mmc</span>, SG 194), (B) transition metal and oxygen environment for Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites and (C) transition metal and oxygen environment for Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites. (D) Honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab viewed down the [001] direction of the big unit cell (honeycomb transition metal superstructure), (E) C<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> stacking sequence viewed in the [110] direction, and (F) C<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>1</sub></small> stacking sequence viewed in the [110] direction. (G) In-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering viewed in the [001] direction at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, (H) at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 and (I) at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3. In (G–I) Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites are characteristic of downward facing triangles (▽) and Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites are characteristic of upward facing triangles (△); sodium on Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites is presented in red colour and sodium in Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites is presented in petrol colour.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Neutron diffraction (ND) is an invaluable tool for investigating transition metal superstructures due to variant scattering lengths of 3d transition metals.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit64"><sup><span class="sup_ref">64</span></sup></a> In particular, the neutron scattering length of Mn = −3.73 fm is distinctly different from that of Ni = 10.3 fm. Thus, ND is optimal for identifying sites occupied by these transition metal cations. For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab is reported, which can be described using a <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t1.gif"></a> superstructure (<span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>22, SG 182 or <span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>, SG 173),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51,60,65</span></sup></a> here noted as a “big unit cell” (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1D–F</a>). The in-plane honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering together with the small and big unit cells is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1D</a>. In the big unit cell, three different transition metal sites, C<small><sub>1</sub></small>, C<small><sub>2</sub></small> and C<small><sub>3</sub></small>, are available, which can be used to describe the stacking sequence of MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs in the <span class="italic">z</span>-direction. For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, nickel atoms are reported to predominantly occupy alternating C<small><sub>2</sub></small> and C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sites in stacked MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs (C<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> stacking)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60</span></sup></a> as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1E</a>. Interestingly, in the isovalent P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the same Mg/Mn honeycomb ordering is reported within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab, however, magnesium atoms are predominantly stacked on top of each other following a C<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>1</sub></small> stacking sequence (<span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>/<span class="italic">mcm</span>, SG 193)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60</span></sup></a> as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1F</a>. Simulated ND patterns for the “small unit cell” neglecting any Ni/Mn ordering and the “big unit cell” with Ni/Mn ordering in C<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>1</sub></small> and C<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> stacking sequences are presented in ESI Fig. S5.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The simulated diffraction patterns clearly differ between <span class="italic">Q</span> = 1.3 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and <span class="italic">Q</span> = 2.4 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> due to the presence and stacking sequence of the Ni/Mn honeycomb ordering.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Within the sodium slab, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings at distinct sodium stoichiometries <span class="italic">x</span> are reported<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1G–I</a>). At <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, sodium ions in Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites arrange in a large-zig-zag (LLZ) around sodium ions in Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1G</a>). At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, the ordering can be described as a row of sodium ions in Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites adjacent to two rows of sodium ions in Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1H</a>). At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, only Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites are occupied, resulting in an altered row ordering (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1I</a>). These Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings are accompanied by distinct potential jumps during galvanostatic (de)sodiation.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44,46</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">For P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a similar crystal structure to that in P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> with a honeycomb Ni/Mn superstructure in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab is reported, however, with additional manganese atoms located on nickel sites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54</span></sup></a> Based on a smoother potential profile and the absence of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy superstructure reflections at <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.62, one report<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54</span></sup></a> concludes that the LLZ-ordering is disrupted in P2-Na<small><sub>0.62</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">In this work, we thoroughly characterize the crystal structure of P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and investigate the extent of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering and the nature of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordered phases in these materials. Furthermore, this report correlates the in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings with transition metal charge ordering arising from the transition metal superstructure in these materials.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect768"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect770"><span class="b_heading">Material synthesis</span></h3>
        <span>Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> were prepared in a three-step synthesis route following co-precipitation of a hydroxide precursor, mixing of the dried precipitate with NaOH as the sodium source and subsequent calcination at high temperature to obtain the final cathode active material (CAM). The dense, spherical Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>(OH)<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>(OH)<small><sub>2</sub></small> hydroxide precursors were prepared under vigorous stirring in a continuously stirred tank reactor (CSTR, volume = 1 liter). The reactor was constantly fed with deaerated, aqueous solutions of Mn(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>·4H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (Carl Roth) and Ni(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>·6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (Carl Roth), NH<small><sub>3</sub></small> (Carl Roth) and NaOH (Carl Roth). The precipitate collected at the reactor outlet was filtered and washed to remove residual salt solutions and dried. Subsequently, these hydroxide precursors were mixed with respective amounts of aqueous NaOH (Sigma-Aldrich) solution and dried at 80 °C following a wet impregnation procedure. The dried mixture was then calcined in a box furnace (Carbolite Gero). Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> was calcined for 10 h at 900 °C in a pure oxygen atmosphere, Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> was calcined for 10 h at 900 °C in air as described elsewhere<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> was calcined for 10 h at 1000 °C in pure oxygen. After passive cooling to 200 °C, the powders were directly transferred into a Büchi glass oven, where they were kept at 200 °C under dynamic vacuum overnight. Subsequently, the CAM powders were transferred without any further contact with ambient air into an Ar-filled glovebox (MBraun, H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O &lt; 0.1 ppm, O<small><sub>2</sub></small> &lt; 0.1 ppm). Consecutive powder handling, electrode preparation and cell assembly were conducted in the same glovebox.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">For powder characterization with ND, a second batch of Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> was prepared based on the same hydroxide precursor and following the same synthesis procedure. A comparison of the characterization results for the two batches is presented in ESI Fig. S6 with ESI Tables S3 and S4 and ESI Fig. S7 with ESI Tables S5 and S6<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> for Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Additionally, P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, P2-Na<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>2/9</sub></small>Al<small><sub>1/9</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Al<small><sub>1/9</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>5/9</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> were synthesized. The respective synthesis route, main characterization results and electrochemical testing procedure are described in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect857"><span class="b_heading">Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>Inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES, Spectro Arcos SOP) with a diluted <span class="italic">aqua regia</span> solution was used for analysis of the chemical composition. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy in attenuated total reflectance mode (ATR-FTIR) was performed using a Bruker Alpha spectrometer with an Alpha-P ATR unit placed in an Ar-filled glovebox. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was used to characterize the powder morphology using a Leo 1530VP (Zeiss) equipped with an Everhart–Thornley SE detector at 5 kV acceleration voltage. Powder XRD patterns were collected on a D8Advance (Bruker) in Bragg–Brentano geometry equipped with a Cu X-ray tube and a LynxEye XE-T detector. Neutron diffraction patterns were recorded at MEREDIT instrument at the Nuclear Physics Institute CAS located in Řež near Prague, Czech Republic. A joint refinement of XRD and ND patterns was performed to obtain lattice parameters, Wyckoff sites and site occupations using the FullProf Suite software.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit66"><sup><span class="sup_ref">66</span></sup></a> Lattice parameters were refined independently for XRD and ND patterns, due to potentially different measurement temperatures and the different sensitivities of X-ray and neutron radiation. Instrumental broadening was determined from SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> powder on a mosaic Cu(222) monocrystal for ND and NIST640c Si powder for XRD. Based on the results of the joint ND and XRD refinement, FAULTS software<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit67"><sup><span class="sup_ref">67</span></sup></a> was deployed to obtain the stacking probability of P3-type stacking faults from the XRD patterns<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> and to analyse the stacking probability of C<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>1</sub></small> stacking faults from ND patterns. The refinement procedure is described in more detail in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Throughout the manuscript, depictions of the crystal structures were prepared using VESTA software.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit68"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68</span></sup></a></span>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <span class="italic">Ex situ</span> capillary transmission XRD measurements were performed on the same D8Advance (Bruker) instrument. For this purpose, CR2032 coin cells were galvanostatically (dis)charged to obtain the desired active material sodium content and subsequently opened inside an argon filled glovebox. After rinsing with dimethyl carbonate (DMC), the electrode composite was carefully scratched off the aluminium current collector foil and filled into glass capillaries (<span class="italic">Ø</span> 0.3 mm, Hilgenberg). The glass capillaries were sealed inside an argon filled glovebox to avoid any contact with the ambient environment.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect872"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical investigations</span></h3>
        <span>Slurries were prepared by homogeneously dispersing a CAM powder, polyvinylidene difluoride binder (PVDF, Solvay Solef 5130) and conductive carbon (SuperP-Li, Imerys Graphite &amp; Carbon) with an 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 weight ratio in an appropriate amount of anhydrous <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (Sigma-Aldrich). These slurries were cast on aluminium current collector foils using the doctor blade technique. Electrodes with <span class="italic">Ø</span>12 mm and a typical active material mass loading of approximately 4 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small> were punched from the dried electrode sheets. The electrodes were further dried in a Büchi glass oven at 130 °C and dynamic vacuum (≈2 × 10<small><sup>−5</sup></small> bar) overnight. Alumina coated CR2032 coin cells (Hohsen) were prepared using these electrodes, two layers of a glass fibre separator (GFA, Whatman, <span class="italic">Ø</span>16 mm), 150 μl of 1 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in propylene carbonate (PC) + 5 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as electrolyte and sodium metal foil as the counter electrode (Acros Organics, <span class="italic">Ø</span>16 mm). The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed on a VMP3 potentiostat (Biologic) using specific current pulses of 17.3 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> for 10 min within the voltage range of 1.5–4.3 V. Equilibrium was assumed when the absolute voltage change was &lt;0.0001 V h<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Entropymetry measurements were performed using the same potentiostat and a programmable temperature chamber (CTS GmbH) with temperature steps at 40 °C, 25 °C and 10 °C. Specific current pulses of 8.7 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with various time periods were applied at 40 °C, followed by a relaxation time of 8 h at the same temperature. In the following, the temperature was changed within 15 min to 25 °C, where it was held for 105 min. Subsequently, the temperature was changed to 10 °C following the same procedure before another current pulse was applied at 40 °C. Equilibrium voltages were derived from the average voltage of the last thirty minutes at each temperature step. The sodium stoichiometry was calculated based on the measured charge of the current pulse and the electrode active material mass, assuming that side reactions were negligible. For every sodium stoichiometry, the entropy change was derived from the obtained slope of the cell voltage with temperature as described in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <span class="italic">Operando</span> synchrotron XRD measurements were performed at PETRA III, beamline P02.1 (DESY, Hamburg, Germany).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit69"><sup><span class="sup_ref">69</span></sup></a> 2D diffraction images were collected using a VATEX CT4343 area detector (2880 × 2880 pixels, 150 × 150 μm pixel size) for 60 seconds. The obtained data were integrated using the DAWN package.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit70"><sup><span class="sup_ref">70</span></sup></a> An eight-fold coin cell holder, connected to a Biologic Instruments potentiostat, was employed as described elsewhere.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit71"><sup><span class="sup_ref">71</span></sup></a> A wavelength of 0.20734(1) Å was determined by analysis of the positions of reflections of the LaB<small><sub>6</sub></small> reference material (NIST 660C). Prior to recording the first diffraction pattern, the electrochemical cells were subject to a formation cycle at 8.65 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (discharge to 1.5 V followed by charge to 4.2 V). Subsequently, diffraction patterns were collected with the pre-charged cell by first discharging to 1.5 V followed by charge to 4.3 V at a constant current of 8.65 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. All diffraction patterns were analysed by the Rietveld method with TOPAS V6 software where the reflections of the Al current collector served as the internal reference.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Throughout this work, either cell voltages from two-electrode measurements with a sodium metal counter electrode or potentials against a sodium metal quasi-reference electrode (three-electrode setup) are reported.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect895"><span class="b_heading">Computational methods</span></h3>
        <span>Periodic first-principles calculations based on density functional theory (DFT)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit72"><sup><span class="sup_ref">72,73</span></sup></a> were performed to determine the convex hull for the Na intercalation for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The projector augmented wave method (PAW)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit74"><sup><span class="sup_ref">74</span></sup></a> was used in conjunction with the PBE<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit75"><sup><span class="sup_ref">75</span></sup></a> exchange and correlation functional. In addition, the Grimme D3 correction<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit76"><sup><span class="sup_ref">76</span></sup></a> was employed as implemented in the Vienna <span class="italic">Ab initio</span> Simulation Package (VASP)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit77"><sup><span class="sup_ref">77–79</span></sup></a> to capture the influence of dispersion effects. The data-driven Hubbard-type <span class="italic">U</span> corrections<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit80"><sup><span class="sup_ref">80</span></sup></a> of <span class="italic">U</span><small><sub>Ni</sub></small> = 6.2 eV and <span class="italic">U</span><small><sub>Mn</sub></small> = 3.9 eV (ref. <a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit81">81</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit82">82</a>) were used to treat the strongly correlated 3d-electrons of the Ni and Mn atoms. The plane wave cut-off was set to 520 eV and a 3 × 3 × 3 <span class="italic">k</span>-point mesh was employed to sample the Brillouin zone. A large supercell containing 24 formula units and ferromagnetic ordering was chosen. The electronic convergence criterion was set to 1 × 10<small><sup>−6</sup></small> eV and the structures were relaxed until all forces converged below 0.01 eV Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The open circuit potential <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>OCV</sub></small> in a Na-ion battery is governed by the change in free enthalpy Δ<span class="italic">G</span> for the transfer of a Na atom. Thus, the open circuit voltage (OCV) is determined by the difference of the chemical potential <span class="italic">μ</span> of Na in the anode and cathode by taking into account the number of transferred electrons <span class="italic">n</span><small><sub>e</sub></small> and the Faraday constant <span class="italic">F</span>:<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t2.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t2.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
        <p class="otherpara">By neglecting <span class="italic">pV</span> and the entropic contributions to Δ<span class="italic">G</span>, the difference in the chemical potential of the Na atom is approximated using the total energy of DFT calculations <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>DFT</sub></small>. The calculation of the <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>OCV</sub></small> for an exemplary layered oxide compound Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> follows:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2"><span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>OCV</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>DFT</sub></small>(Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small>+Δ<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) − (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>DFT</sub></small>(Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) + Δ<span class="italic">xE</span><small><sub>DFT</sub></small>(Na<small><sub>metal</sub></small>)).</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">In <a title="" href="#eqn2">eqn (2)</a>, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>DFT</sub></small> is stated in eV and sodium metal is chosen as the reference for the anode. Since the mode of intercalation in these materials deviates from a simple solid-solution behaviour and first order phase transformations at high potentials as well as Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering transitions occur, the convex hull for the Na intercalation compound is derived to identify thermodynamically stable configurations. The convex hull is constructed from the formation energy <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>fx</sub></small> with respect to the decomposition into the Na deficient O2-type structure (<span class="italic">x</span> = 0) and the P2-type structure at a Na content of <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t3.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t3.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Once the convex hull is constructed by calculating the formation energy per formula unit, the OCV for each stable configuration is obtained using <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>. The convex hull calculations for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> were employed following the previously described procedure for the sodium contents 0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 1/3 (ref. <a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit53">53</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83">83</a>) and by considering the row and large-zig-zag Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings at the Na contents <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, 1/2 and 2/3 as identified by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> and the alternative Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 proposed by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit65"><sup><span class="sup_ref">65,83</span></sup></a> Additional Na configurations for the Na contents <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, 5/12, and 7/12 were selected by minimizing the classical Ewald energy<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit84"><sup><span class="sup_ref">84</span></sup></a> assuming integer charges for redox inactive atoms (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, O<small><sup>2−</sup></small>) and variable charges for the TM atoms (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Ni<small><sup>2+/3+/4+</sup></small>, and Mn<small><sup>3+/4+</sup></small>) for all possible configurations as implemented in pymatgen.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit85"><sup><span class="sup_ref">85</span></sup></a> Overall, a minimum of 10 configurations per Na content were obtained for the Na composition range 0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 1/3 and 20 configurations for the Na composition range 1/3 ≤ <span class="italic">x</span> ≤ 2/3 in steps of <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/12 for each compound. Some of the configurations considered in the DFT calculations for Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> are equivalent to the publication by Daubner <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a></p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect1020"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect1022"><span class="b_heading">Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode active materials were synthesized in our labs, where the reduced sodium content in the latter material is targeted to minimize surface impurities such as NaOH, NaHCO<small><sub>3</sub></small> or Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit86"><sup><span class="sup_ref">86,87</span></sup></a> For Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a small amount of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> is detected with ATR-FTIR, whereas the absence of these surface impurities is confirmed for Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> by ATR-FTIR measurements as presented together with the reproduced batches in ESI Fig. S8.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Elemental analysis with ICP-OES results in Na<small><sub>0.69</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.34</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.66</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.74</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.26</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively, which is in good accordance with the targeted stoichiometries. Based on the knowledge that Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> takes place at a lower potential than Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> redox,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50,87</span></sup></a> we can derive the formal oxidation states Na<small><sub>0.69</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.34</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Mn<small><sub>0.01</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.08</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Mn<small><sub>0.66</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>Ni<small><sub>0.26</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. In both samples, nickel is dominantly divalent and manganese is mainly tetravalent. Particle morphology was characterized using SEM (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2A–D</a>). Both samples consist of secondary particles with a spherical morphology. The crystal structure was characterized using powder XRD and ND. The diffraction patterns for Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2E–H</a>. For both materials, the obtained XRD pattern (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2E and F</a>) can be indexed with the hexagonal space group <span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>/<span class="italic">mmc</span> (SG 194), indicating phase pure P2-type crystalline structures. For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the small reflexes located at <span class="italic">Q</span> = 1.9 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and <span class="italic">Q</span> = 2.0 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> originate from the LLZ Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering (see the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2E</a>). For P2-Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, these superstructure reflexes are absent in accordance with the discrepancy in sodium content (see the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2F</a>). For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a subtle split of the (100) and (2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0031_0304.gif" alt="[1 with combining macron]">0) reflections at <span class="italic">Q</span> = 2.5 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and <span class="italic">Q</span> = 4.3 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the XRD pattern suggests a slight orthorhombic distortion of the hexagonal unit cell. Additionally, in both XRD patterns the broadening of (10<span class="italic">l</span>) reflections hints at stacking faults. The ND patterns of both samples exhibit reflections between <span class="italic">Q</span> = 1.3 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and <span class="italic">Q</span> = 2.4 Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, indicating the presence of a Ni/Mn honeycomb ordering with predominantly a C<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> stacking sequence (compare ESI Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Based on these findings, we performed a joint Rietveld refinement of XRD and ND patterns using the “big unit cell” (<span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>22, SG 182) with a honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering and a C<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> stacking sequence. For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, <span class="italic">C</span>222<small><sub>1</sub></small> (SG 20) was used to account for the slight orthorhombic distortion (<span class="italic">b</span>/<span class="italic">a</span> = 1.728). The results are further refined using “P3-type stacking faults” to account for anisotropic broadening<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> and C<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>1</sub></small> stacking faults. The refined lattice parameters, occupancies and staking fault probabilities are summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Tables 1</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">2</a>. Both materials, Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, exhibit a honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering. For the latter one, this Ni/Mn ordering is partially disrupted as 27% of the nickel site is occupied by manganese. Please note that a different Ni/Mn ordering for the higher Mn content in Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> was evaluated, however, the corresponding simulated ND pattern does not match with the experimentally observed one (ESI Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). To evaluate any potential influence of the sodium content on the Ni/Mn superstructure, a third sample, Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, was synthesized and characterized respectively. The characterization results for Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> are presented in ESI Fig. S10 and ESI Table S7.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> exhibits a similar honeycomb Ni/Mn ordering as Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> with approximately one quarter of the nickel site being occupied by manganese in accordance with the stoichiometry. Therefore, no influence of the sodium content on the Ni/Mn superstructure is observed.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">SEM images of the as-synthesized CAM powders for (A and C) Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (B and D) Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. X-ray diffraction data and Rietveld refinement for (E) Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (F) Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Neutron diffraction data and Rietveld refinement for (G) Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (H) Na<small><sub>0.60</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. In (E–H), the measured pattern, the refined pattern, the difference between those two and the position of the Bragg reflections are presented as red crosses, black lines, grey lines and blue bars, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
