          <hr class="hrule"><br>
        <p class="otherpara">To further elucidate the nature of the M–O bonding, we determined the partial density of states (PDOS) shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8A</a>. Indeed, no Na 2s states are found in the valence band region in agreement with the ionic character of the Na–O bond. In contrast, the M-3d states are found to strongly hybridise with the O-2sp<small><sup>3</sup></small> states in the valence band region, possibly indicating the prevalence of covalent bonding in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer. While this supports the simplified picture of the Na atoms donating their electrons to the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer, it raises the question of the charge density distribution in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. Specifically, it is of interest whether electrons (<span class="italic">e</span><small><sup>−</sup></small>) are entirely localized in M–O bonds or whether they are delocalized over several transition metal atoms. In fact, transition metal compounds are well known to delocalize electrons through direct exchange or super exchange interactions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit106"><sup><span class="sup_ref">106</span></sup></a> In the present case, however, no direct overlap between metal 3d orbitals are possible owing to the long metal–metal distance and the ferromagnetic coupling between ions. Instead, delocalisation is achieved through a super-exchange interaction whereby electrons are delocalized through an at least partially covalent M–O–M two electron three center (2e3c) bond. This is a well known bonding scheme in inorganic chemistry<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit106"><sup><span class="sup_ref">106,107</span></sup></a> which has also been observed for related compounds such as manganese hydroxo complexes<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit108"><sup><span class="sup_ref">108</span></sup></a> and Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit105"><sup><span class="sup_ref">105,109</span></sup></a> These observations suggest that similar bonding characteristics can possibly lead to delocalization of <span class="italic">e</span><small><sup>−</sup></small> also in the here investigated samples.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">(A) The PDOS for the Na contents <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, 1/2, and 2/3 in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t17_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t17.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t17.gif"></a> and Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t18_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t18.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t18.gif"></a> are highlighted in blue and green background colour, respectively. DOS for Mn, Ni and O are depicted in green, blue and red colour, respectively. Total DOS are presented in grey colour. (B) The octahedral crystal field splitting for the transition metal atoms in various formal oxidation states accounting for the Jahn–Teller distortion of Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">For P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8A</a>), independent of Na content, the low spin Ni <span class="italic">t</span><small><sub>2<span class="italic">g</span></sub></small> orbitals are fully occupied in both spin majority and minority directions and are found in the range from −6 eV to 0 eV. The Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t5.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t5.gif"></a> orbitals are either empty (<span class="italic">x</span> = 0) and form the conduction band or are partially occupied (<span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 0) and situated below the Fermi level, depending on the Na content in the structure. During sodiation of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, first the low lying Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t6.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t6.gif"></a> are partially occupied and shifted below the Fermi energy. At a sodium content of <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 the Ni atoms are formally Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and undergo a Jahn–Teller splitting of the <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t7.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t7.gif"></a> into occupied d<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span><small><sup>2</sup></small>−<span class="italic">y</span><small><sup>2</sup></small></sub></small> and unoccupied d<small><sub><span class="italic">z</span><small><sup>2</sup></small></sub></small> states (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8B</a>). This is followed by further reduction of Ni from Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> to Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> with increasing Na content leading to fully occupied Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t8.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t8.gif"></a> states at a Na content of <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3. Independent of the sodium content <span class="italic">x</span> (2/3 &gt; <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 0), Mn <span class="italic">t</span><small><sub>2<span class="italic">g</span></sub></small> orbitals are fully occupied in the spin majority direction and empty in the spin minority direction and are found in the range between −6 eV and 0 eV. The Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t9.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t9.gif"></a> orbitals are situated between 1.5 eV and 2.5 eV independent of the Na content <span class="italic">x</span> and hence do not contribute to the electrochemical processes in this material (0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 2/3). The significant potential jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, as observed in GITT measurements (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3</a>), is qualitatively supported by the observed energy difference between Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t10_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t10.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t10.gif"></a> and Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t11_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t11.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t11.gif"></a> states in PDOS.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, PDOS features appear similar, however, they are shifted in sodium content. Due to the reduced Ni content in Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the respective Ni redox processes are shifted to lower Na contents and Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t12_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t12.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t12.gif"></a> is fully occupied at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2. For <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, the Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t13_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t13.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t13.gif"></a> orbitals are partially occupied and shifted below the Fermi level. With increasing sodium content, the continuously increasing occupation of the Ni <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t14_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t14.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t14.gif"></a> and Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t15_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t15.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t15.gif"></a> can be rationalized considering octahedral crystal field splitting (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8B</a>) in conjunction with the formal oxidation states of the transition metal centre. Thus, sodiation beyond <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 leads to Mn redox activity and the formation of Jahn–Teller active Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> by partial occupation of Mn <a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t16_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-t16.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-t16.gif"></a> states.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To corroborate the observations in the PDOS, we have analysed local magnetic moments <span class="italic">μ</span> and Bader charges of the transition metal atoms and summarized the results in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab3">Table 3</a>. The local magnetic moment allows distinguishing between transition metals of different formal oxidation states based on the number of unpaired electrons expected from the crystal field splitting as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8B</a>. In contrast, Bader charges are determined by charge partitioning of the charge density obtained using DFT and yield qualitative trends for charge situated at the transition metal centre. Despite their fundamentally different methodologies, both methods show similar trends in agreement with the analysis of the PDOS.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">For P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, with increasing sodium content, the local magnetic moment increases and Bader charges decrease for Ni, whereas both values remain invariant for Mn. Hence, with increasing sodium content, Ni is gradually reduced from Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 to Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, while Mn is redox inactive. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> are detected in equal amounts.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">For P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, with increasing sodium content, the local magnetic moment increases and the Bader charges decrease for Ni from <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 to <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, whereas Mn remains invariant within this sodium range. Further sodiation from <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 to <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, leads to increasing local magnetic moment and decreasing Bader charges for Mn, whereas Ni is invariant. Hence, Ni is redox active for <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 1/2 and Mn is redox active at sodium contents <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 1/2.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Overall, the analysis of PDOS, the local magnetic moment μ and Bader charges, suggest that charge within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab is considerably localized at the covalently bonded transition metals, making formal oxidation states an acceptable descriptor for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Please note that similar charge disproportionation has been reported for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>CoO<small><sub>2</sub></small> and O′3-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> with experimental and computational methods.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83,95–97,110–112</span></sup></a></p>
        <p class="otherpara">As described above, various transition metal oxidation states within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab potentially result in low energy sodium sites. The interplay between the preferential occupation of these low energy sodium sites and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion eventually results in Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings. To deconvolute these two effects, we have calculated sodium site energies at a high vacancy limit by placing one sodium atom in a supercell with 24 transition metals (<span class="italic">x</span> = 1/24). The supercell volume was fixed to the one of a calculated, hypothetical empty P2-structure. Subsequently, we analysed the difference in energy between the respective site and the lowest energy site. Since only one sodium atom is placed within the large supercell, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion can be neglected and solely the influence of the immediate environment is modelled. Please note that these structures constitute a model system and do not reflect the experimentally found configurations (P2–O2 two-phase reaction).</p>
        <p class="otherpara">For P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the honeycomb arrangement of the metal atoms distinguishes the Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites in Na<small><sup>Mn–Mn</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small> and Na<small><sup>Mn–Ni</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small> that either sit in between two Mn octahedra (Mn–Na–Mn dumbbell) or in between a Ni and a Mn octahedra (Ni–Na–Mn dumbbell), respectively, while all Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> are equivalent. In P2-Na<small><sub>1/24</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the sodium site energies follow the trend Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> &lt; Na<small><sup>Mn–Ni</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small> &lt; Na<small><sup>Mn–Mn</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small> as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9A</a>. This qualitative trend can be rationalized when considering that the insertion of one Na atom leads to the reduction of one Ni centre from Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small> to Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>. Since the Na–O bond is ionic, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> interacts with the charge density distribution in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab and Na sites in vicinity of Ni centres are beneficial due to their lower formal oxidation state. In P2-Ni<small><sub>1/24</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the substitution of 1/12 of the Ni atoms by Mn atoms leads to further inequivalence of the Na sites when the nearest neighbour transition metals are considered, which is reflected in the sodium site energies as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9B</a>. The Na site energy is substantially increased for Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites in Mn–Na–Mn dumbbells and the surrounding Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites. This phenomenon is caused by the redox-inactive nature of the Mn atoms at such small sodium contents. Mn atoms remain in the formal 4+ oxidation state and therefore lead to significant electrostatic repulsion of the adjacent Na cations. The charge is compensated by a reduction of nearest-neighbour Ni atoms, which are considerably far from the particular high-energy sodium lattice sites. Overall, the calculated sodium site energies suggest preferential occupation of Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites over Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites in accordance with expectations based on Pauling's principles.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a> For P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9A</a>), two energetically different Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites are available, which arrange in a distinct pattern. For P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9B</a>), additional Mn–Na–Mn dumbbells result in an energy penalty for corresponding Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites and adjacent Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites. For P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the simple site energy pattern, as observed for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, is disrupted. In this model experiment, site energies differ in more than 200 mV, suggesting a potentially strong influence of site energies on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig9"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f9.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f9.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 9 </b> <span id="fig9"><span class="graphic_title">Site energies for the high-vacancy limit corresponding to one Na atom per supercell in (A) P2-Na<small><sub>1/24</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and in (B) P2-Na<small><sub>1/24</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The Na atoms are coloured by an energy scale based on the site energy corresponding to the difference in energy of the respective site and the lowest energy site, where grey, yellow, orange and red correspond to the energy ranges 0–50 meV, 50–100 meV, 100–200 meV and &gt;300 meV, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Encouraged by these results, we have analysed the most stable structures for <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3 in terms of charge ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab and its relation to Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering. Please note that the analysed structures are in accordance with the experimental characterization and therefore most likely resemble the physical reality. Respective structure files are provided in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> A depiction of the calculated most stable structures following the in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings as reported by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10A and B</a> for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. For <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, an additional comparison between the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings reported by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> and Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> is presented in ESI Fig. S16.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In both figures, the three-dimensional crystal structure is presented as stacking of three slices with [0 0 1] orientation. Charge ordering within the transition metal slab as derived from magnetic moments and Bader charges (<a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab3">Table 3</a>) is depicted in the colour code. In-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings are presented in red colour for occupied Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites and petrol colour for occupied Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites (following the colour code in the publication by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a>). Occupied sodium sites are categorized by their closest neighbour transition metals and the respective oxidation state.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig10"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f10_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f10.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f10.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 10 </b> <span id="fig10"><span class="graphic_title">Most stable structures following the in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering reported by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> in relation to the respective transition metal charge ordering for (A) P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (B) P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> are presented in blue, green, yellow and magenta colour, respectively. Occupied Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites are presented in petrol colour and Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites are presented in red colour.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Orderings following Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10</a>) – for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, solely Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> next to Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> are observed at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 and 1/3, respectively, resulting in simple hexagonal charge orderings within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> arrange in alternating lines, which is in contrast to a more homogeneous charge distribution within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab following a hexagonal pattern. A similar charge ordering in lines has been observed for Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>CoO<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83,95–97</span></sup></a> For P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the change in Ni/Mn stoichiometry leads to a shift to lower oxidation states as discussed above. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, the addition of manganese to the structure leads to two adjacent Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> atoms. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, solely Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> is present. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, a line ordering of Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> is observed. Overall, the simple charge orderings observed in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> are disrupted in P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. As a result of the simple charge orderings in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites are exclusively occupied if one of the nearest neighbour transition metals is Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>. For each sodium stoichiometry, all occupied Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites exhibit the same transition metal oxidation state environment. For P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the disrupted charge ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab causes more diverse nearest-neighbour environments for occupied Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Ordering following Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> (ESI Fig. S16<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) – for both P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, we find the same transition metal charge orderings for the in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering reported by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> and Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> Independent of the Ni/Mn ratio, two Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> sites with different nearest-neighbour environments (Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small>–Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small><small><sub>f</sub></small>–Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>) and two energetically different Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites are occupied. The nearest-neighbour environment of these Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites is influenced by the Ni/Mn ratio, eventually influencing the stability of the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Comparison of evaluated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 – both <span class="italic">operando</span> XRD experiments and DFT calculations indicate that the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering as reported by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> is present in P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. For P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, no indication for long-range Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is found in <span class="italic">operando</span> XRD experiments and DFT calculations suggest a marginal energy benefit for the ordering reported by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> Please note that the ordering reported by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> incorporates a lower fraction of Na<small><sub>e</sub></small> sites (Na<small><sub>e</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> = 1) compared to the reported in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering by Lee <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> (Na<small><sub>e</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>f</sub></small> = 2). Furthermore, the ordering reported by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> is characteristic for increased mean squared Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> distances, resulting in an energy benefit by minimizing in-plane Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion. For P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the minimized Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion (Huang ordering) seems to prevail over the effect of low energy sodium sites (Lee ordering). For P2-Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the disruption of the transition metal charge ordering destabilizes the ordering reported by Huang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> As a result, none of both Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings results in a significant energy gain and long-range Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is disrupted.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Influence of Ni/Mn stoichiometry on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings – if we compare the same Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordered structure for P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (vertical comparison in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10</a> and ESI fig. S16<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> distances remain constant. Therefore, the relative stability of the compared Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings is solely influenced by the transition metal environment. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, the decrease in the Ni/Mn ratio from P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> to P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> leads to the occupation of energetically more diverse sodium sites. Consequently, the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is partially disrupted in P2-Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2, the transition metal charge ordering in rows in P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is disrupted when the Ni/Mn ratio is increased (P2-Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>). As a result, the energy benefit of the Huang ordering is lost and any long-range Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is suppressed. At <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3, no influence of the Ni/Mn ratio and the transition metal charge ordering on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering is found. Enabled by the relatively low sodium content, only low energy sodium sites are occupied in both materials and we conclude that Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 is dominated by the effect of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect2624"><span class="a_heading">Guideline and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>Based on the above findings, we can deduce that the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in P2-type Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> at sodium contents of <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 and 1/2 are considerably influenced by the charge ordering in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. Therefore, these Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings can be suppressed if the transition metal charge orderings are disrupted. The Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 is dominated by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>–Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> repulsion and therefore is largely independent of charge ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">To crosscheck this hypothesis, we have synthesized P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and P2-Na<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Within the applied potential range (1.5–4.3 V), Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Mg<small><sup>2+</sup></small> remain redox inactive.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113–115</span></sup></a> For P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the expected formal oxidation states (assuming Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and O<small><sup>2−</sup></small>) are Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. According to Delmas <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit58"><sup><span class="sup_ref">58</span></sup></a> the hexagonal transition metal ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab results from the difference in ionic radii during synthesis. Based on the ionic radii (Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> = 0.69 Å, Mg<small><sup>2+</sup></small> = 0.72 Å, and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> = 0.53 Å),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit116"><sup><span class="sup_ref">116</span></sup></a> we anticipate hexagonal ordering of small Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> ions surrounding larger Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> &amp; Mg<small><sup>2+</sup></small> ions. This honeycomb ordering is in accordance with literature reports.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51,60</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">As a result, we expect the following formal oxidation states at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3: Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Ni<small><sub>2/12</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>2/12</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Ni<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (“C<small><sub>1</sub></small> site” in parenthesis). Therefore, a perfect honeycomb charge ordering is expected at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, whereas a disrupted charge ordering is expected for <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 and 1/3. Following our guideline, we expect a potential jump caused by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 and a potential shoulder at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2. As discussed above, no effect of charge ordering on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 is expected. The corresponding first cycle potential profile is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11B</a>. In accordance with literature reports,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51,113,117,118</span></sup></a> the potential profile exhibits a distinct jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 (approx. 3.0 V) and a smoothed jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/2 (approx. 3.5 V). Similar to P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the change from Ni<small><sup>2+/3+</sup></small> to Mn<small><sup>3+/4+</sup></small> redox most likely contributes to the significant voltage jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig11"><a href="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f11_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta04786a-f11.tif" src="/image/article/2025/TA/d4ta04786a/d4ta04786a-f11.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 11 </b> <span id="fig11"><span class="graphic_title">Potential profile for the first two full galvanostatic cycles of (A) P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, (B) P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (C) Na<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> as cathode materials in a sodium half-cell.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">For P2-Na<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>I</sup></small>Mn<small><sup>IV</sup></small><small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the expected formal oxidation states are Na<small><sub>3/4</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>I</sup></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, resulting in a hexagonal ordering of large Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> &amp; Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> (Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> = 0.76 Å)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit116"><sup><span class="sup_ref">116</span></sup></a> surrounded by small Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. In accordance with the literature,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113</span></sup></a> superstructure reflections in the XRD pattern support the honeycomb superstructure within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab (ESI Fig. S17<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). At the relevant sodium contents for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering, the corresponding formal oxidation states are Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>1/6</sub></small><small><sup>II</sup></small>Ni<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>I</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Na<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>1/4</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>I</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>III</sup></small>Ni<small><sub>1/6</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>Li<small><sub>1/12</sub></small><small><sup>I</sup></small>]Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small><small><sup>IV</sup></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Following the guideline above, we expect highly disrupted charge ordering at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3, leading to a considerably smooth potential profile for <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 1/3. The corresponding first cycle potential profile, as presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11C</a>, is smooth and solely exhibits a small shoulder at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 and a slight jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 1/3 in accordance with earlier reports.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113,115,119</span></sup></a> The significant voltage jump at <span class="italic">x</span> = 3/4 is most likely caused by the transition from Ni<small><sup>2+/3+</sup></small> to Mn<small><sup>3+/4+</sup></small> redox.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Further results, obtained by Al substitution for either Ni or Mn in P2-Na<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, are presented in ESI Fig. S19.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Al substitution for Mn is expected to have only minor effects on the transition metal charge ordering in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. Experimentally, we find no significant impact on the potential jumps indicative for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3. In contrast, Al substitution for Ni is expected to disrupt the transition metal charge ordering in the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab. The experimentally found potential profile is significantly smoothened at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3 and 1/2 in accordance with earlier reports.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit120"><sup><span class="sup_ref">120</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">The experimentally observed potential profiles for the undoped, Mg- and Li-doped samples (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11</a>), as well as Al-substitutions (ESI Fig. S19<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), comply with the expectations based on the herein postulated guideline, underlining the significant impact of transition metal charge ordering on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering. The cycling stability of the undoped, Mg- and Li-doped samples increases with decreasing tendency for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering (ESI Fig. S20<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Analogously to the above-discussed materials, the herein postulated guideline also holds for Zn-<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113,121</span></sup></a> and Cu-doping.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113,122–124</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">High entropy materials – lately, “high entropy” materials are explored as promising future cathode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125–130</span></sup></a> Per definition, high entropy materials are single-phase materials consisting of at least five (near)-equimolar species, resulting in an ideal configurational entropy <span class="italic">S</span><small><sup>ideal</sup></small> ≥ 1.609 <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>B</sub></small> per transition metal site, where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>B</sub></small> is the Boltzmann constant.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125,131,132</span></sup></a> In view of this work, the tendency for a smooth potential profile of “high entropy layered sodium transition metal oxides” can be rationalized. The presence of various transition metals with diverse formal oxidation states eliminates long-range charge ordering, which then results in suppressed Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings and a smooth potential profile. Please note that high entropy cathode materials may exhibit further potential advantages, such as minimized strain or enhanced solid diffusion,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125,130,133–138</span></sup></a> which are beyond the scope of this work.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect2839"><span class="a_heading">Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>Within this work, we have demonstrated the significance of charge ordering within the transition metal slab on the presence and extent of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in P2-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Simple charge ordering patterns within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab support the tendency towards Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings. Disrupted charge ordering patterns lead to disrupted or suppressed Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">The herein proposed guideline enables the prediction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings and the associated potential jumps solely on the transition metal composition, the consideration of formal oxidation states and well-known atomic crystal radii. In the first step, the presence of a honeycomb transition metal superstructure is analysed based on either experimental data or by considering the formal oxidation states and ionic radii of the transition metals in the as-synthesized material. In the second step, the formal oxidation states of the transition metals are calculated for sodium stoichiometries <span class="italic">x</span> = 2/3, 1/2 and 1/3. Combining the structural information (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, honeycomb superstructure ordering within the MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slab) with the formal oxidation states enables the prediction of charge ordering and, as a consequence, the tendency for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Besides estimating the tendency for detrimental Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings in a given transition metal composition, this rather simple framework can as well serve to design and optimize cathode compositions with suppressed Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy orderings and therefore improved long-term cycling stability.</p>
